B003U9W3GM|4.0|As a young man, Eugene Sledge joined the US Marines \\"to do his bit\\" in World War 2 and because of his choice of arm, he was sent to fight in the Pacific War against the mighty and tenacious Imperial Japanese Army. \\"With the Old Breed\\" describes his experiences in two of the nastier campaigns in that theatre, Pelelieu and Okinawa (and was one of the references for the HBO mini-series \\"[[ASIN:B003QP47RS The Pacific]]\\").<br /><br />We (in the UK) are perhaps more used to hearing of the European war, in which the combatants treated each other (for the most part) with a good deal of respect, even chivalry. Admittedly the truth may differ somewhat and the non-combatants were dealt with with rather less regard, but we generally consider the European war to have been a \\"civilised war\\". There was nothing civilised about the Pacific war. Lives were thrown away on both sides for posession of tiny coral atolls with a reckless abandon that matches the waste of the Great War. It was fought with a savagery and hate that beggars belief and Sledge pulls no punches in describing the horror; witness the scene is where a fellow marine removes the gold teeth of an injured Japanese soldier. This is not a comfortable book to read.<br /><br />Neither is this a military history. There are notes at the end of each chapter that add a little context, but Sledge wrote the account from the point of view of a private soldier, working from a battle diary that he kept in his pocket bible. He had little or no idea of the big picture and in most cases he had no idea of what was going on, full stop. His perspective comes from over the sights of his carbine; all he can see is the wall of his dugout, the bodies of friends and foe scattered around him. All he can hear is the rattle of rifle fire, the screech and crash of artillery and the screams of the dying. Sledge fully understood his position in all of this - as cannon fodder and no more - but he also understood the horror and waste of what was happening around him and he conveys the brutality and futility very well indeed.<br /><br />Reading some of the other reviews, however, you will see him described as a great writer, even to the extent that he is compared with Robert Graves. I have not read Graves but I am certainly not convinced that \\"With the Old Breed\\" counts as great writing. I don't want to dwell on the point, but the prose is awkward, unpolished, even clumsy in places. It was clearly not written by a professional, or even particularly experienced writer. Does this detract from the story? No, not at all* (or at least, not much), but unfulfilled expectations of literature do detract. However, these shortcomings do lend the story a certain honesty that you won't find in [[ASIN:0330255738 Dispatches]] (a book that I have come to detest for its smug self-importance), a naiivety that you wont find in [[ASIN:0099532816 All Quiet on the Western Front]] and it is, perhaps,  better for it.<br /><br />In the end, this is an eye-opening, gut-churning and deeply humbling account and it is well worth a read.<br /><br />* Perhaps, when we remember wars, we should take off our clothes and paint ourselves blue and go on all fours all day long and grunt like pigs. That would surely be more appropriate than noble oratory... Kurt Vonnegut<br /><br />The review title is from Frank Herbert's Dune seriesning around him and he conveys the brutality and futility very well indeed.<br /><br />Reading some of the other reviews, however, you will see him described as a great writer, even to the extent that he is compared with Robert Graves. B0015DYIH2|1.0|I have been meaning to write a review about this for a long time, considering every time I explain to one of my friends my lack of interest in seeing the fourth movie they always inquire as to why? Welp, because the fourth book was literally 700+ pages of suck.<br /><br />Let me just start by saying that I am in fact a fan of novels Twilight through Eclipse. However, This novel was in fact so bad it flawed the entire series for me. As a reader, it almost felt like a slap in the face, at the lack of care or logic poured into this book. The previous novels in the series I found to be easy reads, I could finish them in a little over a day. Breaking dawn took me over a month, because at one point I put the book down and literally had to convince myself to finish it, by comparing it to \\"ripping off a band-aid\\", but the process of reading this book was not quick and painless. It was brutal and filled with agony. I have no idea why the tone of the novel and Meyer's writings just changed? Her way of attempting to tie in all the stories could have been drastically different. Although it is a work of paranormal YA fiction at what point do you say \\"woah\\". within regards to certain subplots in this novel, (like a certain werewolf and a certain someone's baby). Here is where I start my itemized list of critiques:<br /><br />The writing-Okay, the writing of this novel itself is simply deplorable. Meyer's writing in the first place is a little pretentious; considering it looks like she thumbed through a thesaurus during the entire process, with her going adverb and adjective crazy. Someone had stated in a earlier review that this novel read like a terrible fanfiction, and that hits the nail on the head. I couldn't get over the lack of professionalism or imagery drawn up from Meyer in this novel. Also, Meyer continuously broke her own set of rules, over and over. Like, Bella skipping the newborn phase. why do you ask that Bella skipped the newborn stage? Because Bella didn't want to go through it, so therefore she wasn't going to. It was like some twisted version of selfish (I mean selfish, not self haha) enlightenment. Bella's the golden goose and when it comes to anything Bella might have to do, there is a loophole or an exception.<br /><br />There were so many holes in the plot of this novel, that any lucid reader who wasn't blinded by sparkly vampires would have done the same, and objected like I did. For example, Bella tells Charlie she's getting married and he's pretty much okay with it; umm what? She's 18 and you're supposed to be her father. Get in there and parent! This is just another example as to how Meyer really had no regard for the logic in this novel, she was just trying to pump it out as fast as possible to feed her audience.<br /><br />The characters-Let me say that as a protagonist, Bella falls very short. I have never hated a main character more than I do Bella Swan. She's so prickly and un-relatable it amazes me that the series found as much success as they did with a stick in the mud, like Bella. Also, let me point out why is it that everyone loves her so much? Everyone in the novel loves her so and thinks she's so adorable when I think for a round character she falls flat. I find her impractical, incomprehensible, and down right selfish. Also, very stupid and impulsive; \\"Yes, I think throwing myself off a cliff will help me see a floating glimpse at my Vampire boyfriend, who at the present time wants nothing to do with me.\\" That's from New Moon albeit, but it proves my point. Someone also stated that in Breaking Dawn no one seems to object to Bella by letting her get what she wants, and I couldn't agree more. Edward moves from mysterious and foreboding vampire, to creepily over possessive, to complete and total doormat, come on seriously? And Jacob became some shadow of his former self, did he go off and finally realize he really didn't need Bella? Of course not!  (Spoilers) Jacob becomes determined to stay with Bella until she has her evil hybrid spawn and is turned into a vampire, yet he'll still love her and what not. Oh, but there's a solution. They can abort the baby, and then Jacob can have a baby with Bella the normal way, so Bella can still have a child and be the same old beautiful brown-eyed Bella everyone loves. Oh, and to add to the yuck factor that seems to be a present theme in this book; this is Edward's idea.<br /><br />The Plot lines-The wedding; I can't honestly tell you about the wedding, I just remember Bella wearing something conservative. I find it funny, because as I mentioned before Meyer uses WAY too many adverbs and adjectives with in regards to her descriptions, but one of the many aspects of this novel that all the readers I believe wanted to hear about she doesn't describe in detail, Oh okay. The honeymoon was like sticking a twinkie on a fishing hook, and dangling in front of a fat man on a treadmill. Everyone was waiting for them to have sex, after all the steamy romance scenes between Bella and Edward, I can honestly say I at least expected a vague description of a little rendezvous in Rio, but nope Meyer didn't feel like writing that because she felt it would be inappropriate to her target demographic, so I had to assume like it was the ending of the Sopranos where everything just goes black, but she talks about how when Bella finally becomes a vampire that Bella and Edward have headboard shattering earthquake sex every night, although we can't discuss it all when it comes to the honeymoon, no no! I do agree with her on the moral dilemma of not introducing her younger readers to sex, however Bella becomes pregnant, at the ripe old age of 18. Yes, let's send THAT message to the audience though. Don't show them sex yet inform them it's okay to get married to their boyfriends and have babies as young as 18. Last but now least, the ending with the Volturi. What a giant disappointment, they just came to Forks observed the area and left all but saying,\\"Carry on about your way.\\" I expected some dramatically climactic battle, but nope again, I was sadly mistaken, and please, don't even get me started on the Loch Ness monster baby Bella and Edward have...<br /><br />And that's just the tip of the iceberg when it comes to the flaw of this novel. It was a horrible piece of literature, and will remain collecting dust on my bookshelf.

0061146307|4.0|This is a really nasty little book. Unabashedly so. The orphans, Violet, Klaus and Sunny are subjected to unfortunate events at every turn...exactly what the title promises! The children are brilliant characters as each has their own identity and their own quirks; Violet is an inventor, Klaus a reader and Sunny likes to bite people. This is a small thing, but I liked how the boy was the reader and the girl the inventor; normally that would be the other way around because of the usual gender stereotypes. It was nice to see that inverted. I loved the relationship between the orphans as well; they look out for each other and don't fight and bicker their way through the whole book as you sometimes get in children's books. Sunny was probably my favourite orphan as she is only a baby but has such a great spirit and provides most of the comic relief in the book.<br /><br />Count Olaf is a brilliant villain. He, and his motley crew of sinister misfits, rival Captain Hook and Cruella de Vil for pure unadulterated evil. There is no reason to like the Count at all, so you hate him with a passion. I like characters you can really sink your teeth into and properly hate- it's like being at a pantomime and booing every time the villain takes to the stage. He treats the children with complete cruelty and disregard, and subjects them to filthy conditions, physical abuse and slave duties. Whenever children are involved there is always a question of paedophilic undertones, and certain events and instances in the book do make you squirm in discomfort...such as the Count caressing Violet's face on a few occasions. It only adds to your feeling of desperately wanting to help the orphans and makes you feel even more tense about the potential threat posed by the Count.<br /><br />What I think I really loved most about this book was how it reminded me of books I used to read when I was younger. I adore Roald Dahl and I always remember my Grandma saying to me, 'You shouldn't read those, they always have a nasty streak'. I couldn't tell her that that's why I loved them. I like books where people die and bad things happen and there is a villain of the piece; my favourite book was always Matilda. I like macabre and dark humour, and Lemony Snicket really knows how to do that really well for children. He doesn't patronise them and assume that they want rose gardens and cuddly bears and happy endings. The children in the book are treated with respect by Snicket in that they face real, adult problems and handle them exquisitely. Adults are useless in this book. Not only the Count and his minions, but even their lawyer Mr Poe who ignores their pleas for help and their neighbour Justice Strauss who is too naive to notice their abuse are ineffectual. Even their dead parents, whose Will prevents them from being adopted by someone who isn't a relative, are hindering them. The children have to use their own resources and creativity despite these stupid grown-ups; in the same way that Harry Potter and characters in most Enid Blyton books do. Adults are so caught up in small domestic problems that they ignore the real issues right in front of them.<br /><br />There was only one thing which didn't work for me in this book. I generally don't like to criticise children's books too harshly as I feel I am not their target audience, and what doesn't work for me might work for a child. However, I wasn't a fan of the word definitions that were slipped into the story as I felt they interrupted the flow of the narrative, and were sometimes even a little confusing. Saying that, I can imagine they would be handy if you were reading this story aloud to children as it would clarify hard words for them and maybe even expand their vocabulary.<br /><br />Overall, this book was everything I was expecting and a little bit more. It was well written and memorable, with an outrageously evil villain living in a sprawling run-down Gothic mansion. I know I would have loved these books as a child, and I would definitely recommend these books to any children I meet in the future!ild, and I would definitely recommend these books to any children I meet in the future!

0545123275|4.0|This book was really adorable. I loved Grace and Sam and their story. It was like 'Beauty and the Beast'...but instead of a selfish brute in a lonely mansion, we got a lovely, bookshop-working, raven-haired, yellow-eyed piece of man candy. The narrative alternates between Sam and Grace's point of view, which is something I think really works in romantic stories as it lets you know how each person feels about the other, and doesn't make it seem as though one just really obsessed with the other! It also accentuated the differences between the characters, which was an aspect I really loved. Sam is sensitive and really into poetry and song lyrics and wears his heart on his sleeve...whereas Grace is stoic and unemotional and finds it hard to express herself. I loved that yin/yang aspect of their relationship; it made it much more believable and I could really relate to it from my own experiences!<br /><br />I also liked the whole host of secondary characters and their relationships with Grace and Sam. I especially liked the way neither Grace nor Sam had perfect relationships with their friends and family, as it became a strong bond that they shared. Grace's parents are pretty useless and pay little attention to their daughter. Although they live in the same house, they lead very different lives and her mum and dad seem to shut themselves up in their studio/office and barely say a word to her or seem at all concerned about her. Sam's parents...well...the less said about them the better. Let's just say they took some pretty gruesome drastic measures when they found out their son was a werewolf. I think these relationships help to explain why Grace and Sam have always clung to the idea of each other; despite not knowing each other at all for years before they meet, they watch each other and feel protective of each other. They wanted unconditional love and believed the other person was a way to get it; thankfully, they were right.<br /><br />I also really liked Grace's troubled relationship with her friend Olivia at the beginning of the book. The way Grace felt, as though her friend was slipping away as they got older, and that she was having to work harder and harder to remain close to her friend, was very realistic. I think we've all been in that position where we have been friends with someone for years and then one day realise that we're not sure why we're friends with them anymore. Friends drift apart, especially when you're Grace and you don't have much time for the trivial worries of your friends when all you can think about is staring at a wolf on your back porch.<br /><br />I really loved the setting of this book. I wasn't keen on the name 'Mercy Falls' because it seemed a bit...tacky and lame but I love the woods and how much the woods featured in the book. They were dark and twisted and mysterious and seemed to enfold the characters and houses within them. I like how Grace's feelings towards Sam are projected onto the woods as well; she gazes at them longingly and dreams of living under the golden leaves just as she dreams of Sam himself. The woods are transformed because she always waited for the wolves and wanted to live amongst them, and I think it was what made Sam really familiar to her when she finally saw him in human form- he smelt like the woods and they were a part of both of them.<br /><br />I really liked the emphasis on smell in this novel, too. Grace has an element of wolf in her since she was bitten as a child so, with the prompting of Sam, she gives in to her wolf-heightened sense of smell- a scene in a sweetshop was one of my favourites in the whole book. A lot of the sensuality between Grace and Sam came from them inhaling each others scents, and finding safety and security in the familarity of each other's smell. I loved this as it was very primitive and animalistic as well as being rather sweet and unique.<br /><br />However, I did dislike some aspects of the book. This isn't the most complex of novels, and there is a lot of repetition of similes and metaphors throughout. There are no radical ideas or anything particularly original: there are no huge problems when Grace, a girl who has dreamt about a particular wolf/boy since she was little, finally has her dreams made a reality; he is everything she wanted and dreamed of. I thought it would be more interesting if, initially, she found it hard to reconcile the two ideas she had had of this boy and had more of a hard time getting to know him. But I guess that wouldn't have been as romantic, which is one of the purposes of the book.<br /><br />Another little thing I had a bit of a problem with was something I know my best friend would've hated if she had been reading this book: the focus on eyes. In young adult fiction, there is a tendency to focus on looks and eyes because it is less erotic and sexual then focusing on bodies and body language. Instead, the characters 'read' each others eyes and expressions and observe other characters emotions through looks in their eyes. This gets a bit annoying as it is pretty unrealistic...how often can you say you have read something just from someone's eyes!? It takes the whole face at least!<br /><br />I also found Sam's song lyrics pretty cheesy. I know some people love all the deep and meaningful stuff but, for me, the song lyrics were a step too far. I could handle him reading her poetry. I could handle him calling her 'angel' (even though I did cringe). But beyond that I did find myself smirking at the cheese...especially because Grace isn't the kind of character who you would think would appreciate that sort of smushy-ness!<br /><br />Despite the few down sides, I did really, really enjoy this book. It was cute and did choke me up a little in places! I really believed in the characters, as I felt they were multi-dimensional and more complex than average. I will definitely be reading the sequel!nd there is a lot of repetition of similes and metaphors throughout. There are no radical ideas or anything particularly original: there are no huge problems when Grace, a girl who has dreamt about a particular wolf/boy since she was little, finally has her dreams made a reality; he is everything she wanted and dreamed of. It takes the whole face at least!<br /><br />I also found Sam's song lyrics pretty cheesy. I know some people love all the deep and meaningful stuff but, for me, the song lyrics were a step too far. The Canadian-American film is an instant cult classic like the 80s Sam Raimi films starring great Bruce Campbell. It has interesting characters, clever (and crazy) jokes and capable acting, but its approach to the subject matter is rather an orthodox one.<br /><br />The film, first-time directed and co-written by Eli Craig, is about Tucker (Alan Tudyk) and Dale (Tyler Labine), two good-natured guys mistaken for \\"cannibalistic mountain people\\" by college students. When Dale saves Allison (Katrina Bowden), who fell into the river, these students, who must have seen \\"Wrong Turn\\" and \\"The Hills Have Eyes,\\" jump to the conclusion: she is being kidnapped! Led by Chad (Jesse Moss), who obviously has some reason to be enthusiastic, the students attempt to \\"rescue\\" Allison. Of course, their mission goes terribly wrong.<br /><br />Part satire and part slapstick, \\"Tucker & Dale vs Evil\\" makes a good use of familiar horror movie elements - a rundown cabin in the woods, a chainsaw-wielding killer, etc. - and turn them into blood-soaked gags. Those jokes may lose their humor if overdone, but the film quickly moves on before this happens, wrapping the story up in a more conventional but satisfactory way.<br /><br />To me, the most impressive thing about the film is characters, especially Tucker and Dale, a horror version of Laurel and Hardy. Well-meaning Allison and her therapy session is also nice.

1400079276|5.0|Internationally bestselling author Haruki Murakami is well known for his use of the fantastic in his novels, to the point where they could quite easily be classed as fantasy or science fiction books; at the very least the literary world likes to brand them as works of \\"magical realism.\\"  Kafka on the Shore is an excellent example of this and a good book to start on for those not too familiar with his work, as the world is well grounded before the fantastic appears in the story, broken up into shortish chapters so the reader doesn't become lost.  The book also features one of the darkest and most horrific scenes involving cats that I've ever read.    There are two storylines in Kafka on the Shore.  One is of Kafka Tamura, who is sick of the pathetic excuse for parenting from his celebrity father, and runs away from home, embarking on his own adventure, meeting special and unusual people only Murakami could create.  He is in search of the identity of his lost sister, and looking to find out who his real mother is.  Kafka ends up working at a small library, where there is a middle-aged lady who could well be his mother, as well as a transsexual librarian who becomes a good and close friend.    And then there is Nakata, an old simple man who seems to be losing his marbles, but actually knows what he's talking about and has his own journey to go on and complete.  He can also talk to cats.  After dealing with a problem he heads on his journey that skillfully brings him into the Kafka storyline, but it is not until near the end of the book that the whole story is revealed and realized.  While the story continues on a little too long, even though the story feels complete, Kafka on the Shore is a great example of the fantastic writer that is Haruki Murakami. 1451627289|5.0|In Stephen King's latest tome, weighing in at almost 850 pages, the master of horror takes on a whole new sub-genre that he hasn't really dealt with before: time travel. What's interesting is while playing around with time travel can be a lot of fun for a while, King sticks to simple rules, and has more fun in making 11/22/63 more of an excellent work of historical fiction.  The date -- November 11th, 1963 -- is one burned in many people's minds, especially if they were alive and old enough at the time to remember where they were the day President John F. Kennedy was assassinated in Dallas, Texas.    Jake Epping is a thirty-five year old high school teacher living in Lisbon Falls, Maine.  He enjoys his simple life, conveying to kids not just the beauty of the English language, but discussing and enlightening the teenagers with some of the great works of literature.  In the opening of 11/22/63, the reader learns about Harry Dunning's past life.  Dunning is an adult student who got his high school diploma a while ago; Jake still has that very special essay Dunning wrote.  It wasn't grammatically correct, and was filled with spelling errors; but it was also the story of the day Dunning's father came home drunk, when he was a child, and brutally murdered his mother, sister and brother with a hammer, while Dunning barely made it out alive with his life, suffering a smashed leg.    It was a moving story that Jake has never forgotten.  He enjoys his days after school going to see his friend Al, who owns a local diner, where he enjoys one of the most delicious burgers on the planet, and the amazing thing about it is he hasn't raised his prices in decades.  A customer can still enjoy a burger with fries for the ridiculously cheap price of under $3.  It seems like something Jake should be suspicious about, but the burgers taste too damn good.  The following day Jake meets up with Al again and finds him to be a changed man, incredibly aged overnight and he looks like he's dying; that's when Al tells him his story.    In the back of his diner is a portal to 1958.  Al himself has been back a number of times, and each time he comes back through it to the present, everything resets.  He's narrowed everything down to one important event he believes will change everything: the assassination of JFK.  He tried once, spending five years back then, but it didn't work.  Now it's up to Jake.  And just to prove that anything is possible, Jake's first mission is going to be to go back and stop Dunning's father from killing his family.    11/22/63 isn't a time travel science fiction novel, it's a historical fiction novel that features time travel; kind of like how The Time Traveler's Wife was a love story involving time travel.  And while the time travel is an entertaining facet of this novel, it is the moving story of the past, of the 1950s and early `60s, the lives of some incredible characters, and the work of one man's detailed effort to prevent one of the most infamous moments in American history.  King has outdone himself with some immense research, immersing the reader in the feel of this time period, in the minutiae of everyday life for some people living in Texas.  And as the reader reads further into the book, he or she can't not notice the details of this world, and how different it is to our modern one.  The result is a moving story with powerful characters and an insight into a way of life many of us have never experienced, making it one of King's most important works of his career. B004LROUW2|4.0|Bestselling Japanese author Haruki Murakami returns with his next encapsulating novel, and this one's a long one.  Originally released as three separate serialized books in Japan, 1Q84 has been released as a 944-page behemoth in the US.  It is the year 1984 and the main character, Aomame makes the decision and exits a taxicab on a gridlocked freeway and goes down an emergency escape staircase and finds herself transported to an alternate world of sorts, though it is still very similar to our own; she comes to call it 1Q84, where \\"Q\\" is Japanese for ?.    Aomame is a special kind of person; technically an assassin who has developed a way of stabbing a person at a specific point in the back of the neck with a small ice-pick type implement that immediately kills the person and makes it almost impossible to rule as anything other than a heart attack.  Aomame's targets are vicious, cruel man who have been bad to their spouses or children or other people for a long time and don't deserve to live in this world.    Then there is the other main character, a writer named Tengo, who is rewriting a most unusual novella penned by a seventeen year-old girl with the goal of having it win a literary prize and become a bestseller.  It is a most unique tale about these \\"little people\\" who create an air chrysalis, which is the title of the book.  Only, once the book becomes hugely successful, Tengo learns more about the origin of the story from the author, as well as the fact that these little people may very well be real in this 1Q84.    Aomame doesn't notice much different with the world of 1Q84, except that the police wear different uniforms and carry automatic machines guns, and also come nightfall there are two moons that rise up: one that looks just like ours, and another smaller misshapen one that rises shortly after it.  This is also a world where the little people exist too, though only Tengo knows about that.  And yet, under Murakami's skillful hand, the two are destined tomeet and be together, after their previous involvement when they were children; it will take time, but the question remains whether it will be soon enough before the little people carry out their own special plan.    1Q84 has many of the elements that Murakami fans have come to expect from him, with his unique characters that draw you in, a crazy world that makes you feel like you're playing a Japanese horror game in some ways, and a writing style that will sweep you along.  A weak point with the book is that while it was originally three books, it could've used some editing, which is always a risk with long works that span multiple volumes, and it slows the pace, detracting from the story.  Nevertheless, 1Q84 is a darkly enchanting novel that will suck you in and not let you go until you are satiated at the end, where a number of questions are answered, though of course, not all, for something must be left to the imagination, otherwise it wouldn't be a true Murakami novel. B0053JJLLC|3.0|Found as an incomplete manuscript on the late Michael Crichton's computer, Micro is an example of the old style of Crichton's work, with a great extension of cutting edge science, pushing it into the field of science fiction.  In some of Crichton's more recent novels there has been an overbearing philosophy and biased political angle; fortunately, there is little of this in Micro, though his \\"corrupt\\" characters are thin and painfully obvious.  Richard Preston, author of The Hot Zone and The Demon in the Freezer, was brought in by the publisher to complete the manuscript, and he does a good job of making the whole novel feel seamless, working off of Crichton's outlines, notes and research.    Micro opens with the scene of three unknown bodies found in a business office in Honolulu; cause of death is uncertain at first, and then attributed to a number of micro cuts and lacerations over the whole body, including, when it is investigated, inside the body on tissues and organs.  Cut to Nanigen Technologies, an up and coming company with a number of secret projects going on.  Seven graduate students are picked from MIT to become assistants for the company located in Hawaii, but stumble onto some details they shouldn't know anything about.  Before they know it, they find themselves shrunk down to just inches in size and abandoned in a rain forest arboretum, left to die.  The question is whether they can first keep themselves alive at this size, with everything out to get them, and then get themselves back to normal size and stop the people behind all this.    Micro definitely has its high points, and while the characters can seem predictable and shallow, overall it's an entertaining novel that doesn't hold up to any of Crichton's greats, like Jurassic Park and Congo, but is nevertheless a fun last book from this bestselling author. B005TMSN4E|2.0|This product works as promised, more or less, but is fundamentally problematic with shorter focal length lenses. The crop factor of the CX sensor on the 1 series makes using F-mount lenses great for telephoto or macro work, but at wide angle focal lengths with large apertures, I've realized that using F-mount lenses with a 1 series camera doesn't really make much sense.<br /><br />I bought this adapter because I wanted to use fast F-mount primes to get some nice bokeh effects, which are not really possible with the 1NIKKOR lenses currently available (as of April 2012). However, having tried it with the Nikon F-mount 50mm and 35mm 1.8g lenses, I was disappointed with the results. With the aperture wide open, images are fairly blurry and there appears to be severe chromatic aberration (to the extent that the built-in CA algorithms in Lightroom 4 can't even clean it up). Additionally, focusing is very slow, often inaccurate, and only works in single-point single-servo mode.<br /><br />But worst of all, even with a 35mm, the crop factor multiplier means you're working well into the telephoto range, so you have to stand WAY back from your subject. For the kinds of picture taking I wanted to do (portraits and up-close shots of things like food, drink, and plant life, with lots of background blur), this is at best awkward and frequently impossible. (I find myself bumping my back against walls!) Sure, it may be possible to get in closer with a 24mm or 28mm lens, but suddenly we're talking about *really* expensive lenses at that point ($800-2000), which also would add a lot of heft to a camera whose very raison d'tre is to be portable.<br /><br />This *adapter* alone is roughly half the price of Nikon's entry-level DSLR. You'd get much better results with that and a 35mm 1.8g lens for the type of photography I describe above. Save the V1 for popping into your backpack or purse for shots of opportunity while out and about. However, until Nikon comes out with some fast primes native to the 1NIKKOR system (they have been promised, but no release dates yet), for bokeh-filled shots in the wide-angle or normal ranges I'm afraid a DSLR is your only option at the moment.s native to the 1NIKKOR system (they have been promised, but no release dates yet), for bokeh-filled shots in the wide-angle or normal ranges I'm afraid a DSLR is your only option at the moment.

0131103628|5.0|Over a decade ago, I dropped an introductory programming class in C because I couldn't understand this book. I was an absolute beginner, and this book was impenetrable. Now, with the benefit of subsequent experience, this text is clear as day.<br /><br />The K&R is a programming classic, and with good reason. It's a succinct guide, only 274 pages, which clearly illuminates the language without excess verbiage or other distractions which have turned many modern programming texts into 1000 page tomes.<br /><br />Although the C language is clearly described and explained, you'll find precious little hand holding here since this book is written primarily for the programmer who is new to C, and not for the absolute beginning programmer. In fact, you won't even find answers to the exercises in this book. For that you'll need \\"The C Answer Book\\" by Tondo & Gimpel, which not only gives the answers, but, more importantly, explains them.<br /><br />[[ASIN:0131096532 The C Answer Book: Solutions to the Exercises in 'The C Programming Language,' Second Edition]]<br /><br />Nevertheless, for the experienced programmer, or the highly motivated beginner, this book delivers, and its diligent study is well rewarded. Be aware, however, that maximum benefit from this book can only be derived by completing all the exercises which are an integral part of the text's tutorial approach.<br /><br />Once you've studied the text, completed all the exercises, and understood the answers, you'll be well on your way to the mastery of C. I therefore give this book my highest possible recommendation.

B0000A0DRY|5.0|Jefferson Airplane's second studio album was recorded October 31 - November 22, 1966 in RCA Victor's Music Center, Hollywood, California and released in February 1967 in both mono and stereo versions. This is the stereo version with six bonus songs (actually 7 as one's a hidden bonus song). This was Grace Slick and Spencer Dryden's first album with the band. The Grateful Dead's Jerry Garcia was responsible for the album title as well as appearing on several of the songs although producer Rick Jarrard denies this. Given the fact that members of the Airplane say he was on there, he's on outtakes, and you can clearly hear his early '67 sound. Jerry Garcia plays electric on Today, acoustic on Comin' Back To Me and Plastic Fantastic Lover. The Grateful Dead also recorded their first album about a month later in the same studio with the same engineer who also produced the Dead's first album. The album, Surrealistic Pillow, represents the counterculture movement of the 1960's but has held up surprisingly well and much of it is still feels very relevant today.<br /><br />The albums starts off with Marty Balin/Jorma Kaukonen's She Has Funny Cars complete with a driving beat, great vocals, sharp guitars and Jack Casady's wonderful fuzz bass. Somebody To Love is a song that Grace brought with her from her last band The Great Society which would become one of the Airplane's biggest hits still turning up on the radio today. My Best Friend was written by Skip Spence who left and would help form Moby Grape. Marty Balin/Paul Kantner turn in a beautiful song called Today which gives way to Marty Balin's wonderful Comin' Back To Me with Grace contributing a pretty recorder part to the song. Marty Balin's 3/5 of a Mile in 10 Seconds starts the second half of the album off on a flying start. Paul Kantner turns in one of my favorites of his with D.C.B.A. -25. How Do You Feel features nice group vocals with Grace turning in another nice recorder part. While I do like later Airplane albums too, I do wish they would've included more of the acoustic/recorder elements we hear here. Speaking of acoustic, Jorma's Embryonic Journey is nothing short of amazing! Grace turns in White Rabbit inspired by Alice In Wonderland and...well...other things. It has a nice electric Spanish feel and would become the band's other best known song. Marty's Plastic Fantastic Lover starts with a slashing acoustic guitar before giving way to a driving beat.<br /><br />The bonus songs here have mostly appeared elsewhere but actually do fit well here as they're from the same sessions. In The Morning, J.P.P. McStep B. Blues (both with Jerry Garcia) and Go To Her appeared on 1974's Early Flight. Come Back Baby is quite good but not as drawn out as it would be later on. The mono single versions of Somebody To Love and White Rabbit are next with an instrumental version of D.C.B.A.-25 as a hidden bonus track. I've noticed that some people have commented on the sound quality of this edition but it sounds very good to me. The original album production always had a bit of echo and the instruments were probably recorded mostly live as they were back then with the vocals then overdubbed. This isn't quite as noticeable on vinyl but stands out more on cd because of higher resolution. This was probably recorded on either a two track machine or three track at most, which limits what can be done, restoration wise.s too, I do wish they would've included more of the acoustic/recorder elements we hear here. Speaking of acoustic, Jorma's Embryonic Journey is nothing short of amazing! Grace turns in White Rabbit inspired by Alice In Wonderland and...well...other things. This was probably recorded on either a two track machine or three track at most, which limits what can be done, restoration wise.

B005ZOBNOI|2.0|Boy, do I have a feeling I'm going to provoke a lot of anger among the Nerdfighter community with this. All I ask is that you hear me out.<br /><br />I've read all of John Green's full-length, independently written novels (opting to skip all the collaborations and side projects). I find him to be a mostly charming, witty writer, but I've never obsessed over him the way his impressive legion of fans (called Nerdfighters) have. Clearly, I've enjoyed him enough to keep checking in every time he's published a new book, but there have been enough flaws or annoyances to keep me a little jaded about the experience. The Fault in Our Stars may have officially tipped the scales against Green for me. It's the first time I've really found myself irritated by a Green novel.<br /><br />I'm going to insert a disclaimer here before anyone tries to accuse me of lacking sensitivity for people who are terminally ill. Both of my parents have had, or still have, cancer at the time of this writing. My partner lost his mother to cancer. I also had a beloved aunt who spent the first twenty-three years of my life fighting recurrences of cancer before succumbing (in fact, I was never actually supposed to meet her, since she was given roughly six months to live upon her initial diagnosis--which was six years before I was even born). I've worn a Live Strong bracelet on my wrist every day for the last seven years to remind me of their struggles and the importance of really living every single day. Furthermore, I count [[ASIN:0571198775 Wit: A Play]] among my favorite books and Terms of Endearment reduced me to emotional rubble (seriously, I can't even watch it. I cried so hard I made ludicrous, hiccupy snuffling noises that made me glad no one was there to witness it).<br /><br />So please believe me when I say that when I found the protagonists (Hazel Grace Lancaster and Augustus Waters) of The Fault in Our Stars irritating, it had nothing to do with their illnesses and everything to do with the way Green constructed their personalities. I'll get to my problems with the plot itself at the end.<br /><br />Green has an upstanding history of creating lovable nerds. He is an author who really understands and celebrates the underdog--the so-called \\"losers\\" of the world. It's why his fan community is known as Nerdfighters (not to mention why he has such a massive fan community at all). He's subverting the paradigm that being intelligent or \\"weird\\" is wrong (if Glee were capable of consistent storytelling it would be spreading the same message on TV every week). But here's the thing: in all of his other books I've believed in his over-articulate outcasts without an incredible suspension of disbelief. I don't believe in Hazel and Augustus the same way. Their dialogue is at best contrived, at worst cloying and, well, ridiculous.<br /><br />I chalk this down to the fact that Hazel and Augustus are worth more to Green as Metaphors than as characters. Everything they do has to be Significant and Important and Meaningful. The main plotpoint, where they embark on a quest to find the author of Hazel's favorite book (which famously lacks closure) so they can find out what happens after the last sentence, is a rather obvious metaphor for their fear of death. For goodness' sake, Hazel can't even eat breakfast without opining about how eggs are the victims of typecasting. \\"I mean seriously,\\" she intones, \\"How did scrambled eggs get stuck with breakfast exclusivity? You can put bacon on a sandwich without anyone freaking out. But the moment your sandwich has an egg, boom, it's a breakfast sandwich ... I don't want to have 'breakfast for dinner' ... I want to have scrambled eggs for dinner without this ridiculous construction that a scrambled egg-inclusive meal is breakfast even when it occurs at dinnertime.\\"* Do you get it, you guys? THE EGG IS A METAPHOR. I just blew your mind, didn't I? It's OK, take a second if you need to.<br /><br />Augustus is, if possible, even moreobviously cratered with Metaphors. His desire to die heroically is repeatedly signified by his obsession with sacrificing his virtual life in video games instead of winning the game (then, irritatingly, Green feels the need to explain it toward the end anyway Just In Case You Missed It). He's the kind of guy who likes to keep an unlit cigarette between his lips, and will gladly explain it's metaphorical significance to you if you ask politely (\\"You put the killing thing right between your teeth, but you don't give it the power to do its killing.\\"), prompting Hazel to remark that he chooses his behaviors based on their metaphorical resonance and Fall Irrevocably In Love With Him.<br /><br />And there's the other problem I have with Augustus and Hazel: their romance feels like a plot construction far more than it feels like a natural stars-crossed Passion For The Ages. In Green's other books, I believed that Miles fell for self-destructive Alaska, I believed that Margo Roth Spiegelman captivated Quentin Jacobsen enough to make him search for her after she disappeared (in [[ASIN:0142402516 Looking for Alaska]] and [[ASIN:014241493X Paper Towns]] respectively). There was something about their force-of-nature presence that made it believable that someone like Miles or Quentin would be lured in. But Augustus Waters just shows up in Hazel's cancer support group and stares at her like Creepy McCreeperson and she just swoons at him. I'm sorry, that's almost as bad as Bella Swan falling in love with Edward Cullen even though he apparently hates her and is stalking her outside her bedroom window. Green attempts to play it cool by having Hazel recognize that she'd be creeped out if it were an ugly guy staring at her, but that doesn't make their love affair any less sudden or any less inexplicable. The plot won't work if they aren't in love, so reason be damned, and there it is: love at first sight.<br /><br />And that brings us to my problems with the plot itself.<br /><br />Observation: repeatedly beginning lines of dialogue in which your characters are supposed to make an Important Observation about Life As We All Know It with \\"Observation: ...\\" gets old, especially when the Observation(:) is as trite as \\"standing in line is a form of oppression.\\" Observation: Random Acts of Capitalization don't make your writing endearing or quirky. Please To Stop.<br /><br />Nevermind the fact that all the peripheral characters are completely flat (definitely an accusation I never expected to make about John Green)--if they don't matter to the author, why should they matter to us? That the plot is dully predictable is a greater offense to me. The \\"plot twist\\" recurrence, the acceptance of fate, the lessons learned by the protagonist at the end ... you see it all coming a mile away.<br /><br />The strength of The Fault in Our Stars is that it refuses to offer false comfort regarding a subject matter that does not have any happy ending: we are all going to die, but we live our lives pretending that words like \\"forever\\" or \\"always\\" have meaning (think of Isaac's subplot in re: the latter word). I suspect this is a new concept for many people; maybe that's why it resonates with so many readers. Perhaps I'm more morbid than your average person, but it was nothing new for me. And it's been done better (the aforementioned Wit for one). The Fault in Our Stars wants to have it both ways: it wants to reflect the harsh realities of impermanence and death, but it wants to leave the reader feeling good in the end. Consider that the book Hazel loves so much is lauded for remaining honest through its complete lack of closure, but Green provides us with a full denouement and conclusion. It derides cheap sentimentality, then uses it as a crutch to make the reader Feel Something. In the end you just can't have it both ways, and that's why The Fault in Our Stars is a failure to me.<br /><br />Grade: D+<br /><br />*\\"breakfast exlusivity?\\" WHO TALKS LIKE THAT?! You can tell me that these kids have been forced into early adulthood due to their circumstances until you're blue in the face and I won't buy it. I'm well into adulthood right now and have never heard anyone, ever, talk in such a contrived manner.servation: repeatedly beginning lines of dialogue in which your characters are supposed to make an Important Observation about Life As We All Know It with \\"Observation: ...\\" gets old, especially when the Observation(:) is as trite as \\"standing in line is a form of oppression.\\" Observation: Random Acts of Capitalization don't make your writing endearing or quirky. Please To Stop.<br /><br />Nevermind the fact that all the peripheral characters are completely flat (definitely an accusation I never expected to make about John Green)--if they don't matter to the author, why should they matter to us? That the plot is dully predictable is a greater offense to me. The \\"plot twist\\" recurrence, the acceptance of fate, the lessons learned by the protagonist at the end ... you see it all coming a mile away.<br /><br />The strength of The Fault in Our Stars is that it refuses to offer false comfort regarding a subject matter that does not have any happy ending: we are all going to die, but we live our lives pretending that words like \\"forever\\" or \\"always\\" have meaning (think of Isaac's subplot in re: the latter word). I suspect this is a new concept for many people; maybe that's why it resonates with so many readers. Perhaps I'm more morbid than your average person, but it was nothing new for me. And it's been done better (the aforementioned Wit for one). The Fault in Our Stars wants to have it both ways: it wants to reflect the harsh realities of impermanence and death, but it wants to leave the reader feeling good in the end. Consider that the book Hazel loves so much is lauded for remaining honest through its complete lack of closure, but Green provides us with a full denouement and conclusion. It derides cheap sentimentality, then uses it as a crutch to make the reader Feel Something. In the end you just can't have it both ways, and that's why The Fault in Our Stars is a failure to me.<br /><br />Grade: D+<br /><br />*\\"breakfast exlusivity?\\" WHO TALKS LIKE THAT?! You can tell me that these kids have been forced into early adulthood due to their circumstances until you're blue in the face and I won't buy it. I'm well into adulthood right now and have never heard anyone, ever, talk in such a contrived manner.

B004INTNH6|5.0|Hallmark of the U.K. is fast taking the lead in re-issuing on CD classic vinyl LPs from the past such as Bye Bye Baby/I Don't Want To Take A Chance by Mary Wells and The Soulful Moods Of Marvin Gaye. Here they delve into the Jazz genre which, in its many forms since the advent of R&R in the mid-1950s, was never a genre which saw a single routinely climb into the higher regions of the various Pop charts, unlike Country and R&B which boasted frequent cross-overs from their devoted charts.<br /><br />But every now and then a pure Jazz tune would score high, as  evidenced by hits by the likes of: bongo player/percussionist Mongo Santamaria, pianists Andr&eacute; Previn, Ray Bryant and Count Basie, organists Jimmy Smith, Richard \\"Groove\\" Holmes and Jimmy McGriff, saxophonists Moe Koffman, Stan Getz, Julian \\"Cannonball\\" Adderley, Les Elgart and Johnny Dankworth, guitarist Charlie Byrd, drummer Cozy Cole, and trombonist Chris Barber.<br /><br />Add to that illustrious group the name of pianist Dave Brubeck who, leading his quartet (bassist Eugene Wright, alto saxophonist Paul Desmond and drummer Joe Morello), recorded this great album at Columbia's 30th Street Studios in New York in June, July and August of 1959 and when it was released it not only drew high praise from the usual Jazz publications, but struck a chord with a buying public that spread way beyond the small but devoted Jazz fan base. So much so that, in 1961, Columbia release Take Five - done in 5/4 time - as a single b/w Blue Rondo A La Turk (Columbia 41479) and saw it go as high as # 25 Billboard Pop Hot 100 that September.<br /><br />That tune in fact, with its clever solos by members of the Quartet, led to a whole new appreciation of Jazz amongst non-traditional audiences, with many more hits to follow by the artists mentioned above. And you'll love Paul Desmond's sax on Everybody's Jumpin' as well as the magic of Wright and Morello.<br /><br />The sound quality is excellent, there are liner notes by Dave Brubeck and renowned British Jazz pianist/historian, the late Steve Race, and the price is right.<br /><br />Dave, now 92, and Eugene \\"The Senator\\" Wright, now 89, are both still with us, while Paul Desmond passed away at age 53 on May 30, 1977 from lung cancer, and Joe Morello at age 82 on March 12, 2011, apparently from natural causes. 6305882592|5.0|Harold and Maude is a hilariously dark, well-shot excursion into figuring out how to live. Wes Anderson borrowed basically his entire style from this movie. The dark tinge, the slow motion, the brilliantly choreographed indie soundtrack. The two leads in this film are pitch-perfect, particularly a young and beguiling Bud Cort whose suicide attempts are movie comic gold (a weird sentence, I know). Take one instance when his mother organizes a blind date. Mommy dearest and the date are foregrounded in the ornate dining room as Harold wanders around in the yard beyond, covering himself in gauze and gasoline and apparently lighting himself ablaze. The date reacts with appropriate horror just as Harold emerges to shake her hand. The scene doesn't sound funny on paper, but it is a gut-splitter. So too, Harold floating in a pool, Harold methodically loading a handgun as his Mom fills out dating paperwork for her pious preferences. And who can forget Harold and Maude meandering from funeral to funeral? Maude is a free-spirit, an ancient whimsical creation despite horrors of her past explained in a flash late in the film. She is a perfect counterpart to Harold. They are arguably the best on-screen couple in history. The only scene that is a bit too wacky is the scene in which Harold eludes his war-hawk uncle by seemingly murdering Maude (this is never mentioned again and seems slightly cartoonish that he got away with it scott-free). This minor snafu aside, the film is a wonder, beautiful and grim in equal measure.  Any fan of Wes Anderson owes it to themselves to see it, and see it more than once.

0765316994|5.0|REDSHIRTS' premise, on the face of it, is comedic. It makes me remember the exchange from Big Bang Theory, that we know we're not in the Matrix because the food would taste better. In this case, we know we're not in the real world because the guy in the red shirt ALWAYS dies on the away mission.<br /><br />It's a fun, fast, satisfying read - but then, something happens. The ending, much as in life, is NOT the ending. Three Codas examine the other side of the story, the effect of the fictional world on the real world. How fiction informs it, reforms it, improves it. It's in the Codas that REDSHIRTS becomes more than a nifty logic puzzle with pulse guns. In the end, it's a thank you to the people who imagine - and who share their imaginings with a world in need of them. While they're not our creators (probably!) they do more for us than they, or we, can ever know.

0439023513|5.0|SPOILERS AHEAD!!!<br /><br />Like many others, I absolutely loved the first two books. While reading Mockingjay I was at first very put off by how the book was unfolding- slow, jumpy, Katniss- our super strong hero- becoming a pawn and sedated over and over. It's not how we want our hero to be. But what can we realistically expect from a hero who has lived through the trauma and violence that she has? Her behavior, and that of the other characters, brought back very real memories for me. A close relative of mine was sent to Iraq while he was in the Army. When he came back, many of his behaviors (nightmares, blackouts, hallucinations, etc.) are what Katniss and the other characters experience in the book. I realized that the book, and the actions of the characters, makes sense. This is what happens when people are put through unspeakable horrors. That's the point of the book, to make us feel uncomfortable and put off. We are meant to be nauseated by war. We are meant to abhor violence. It's meant to make us take a serious, hard look at the world.<br /><br />I personally think Suzanne Collins was brilliant in how she did this- we all fell in love with Katniss, and we want her to be happy and have the fairy tale ending. So at the end when her world is destroyed, we should be mad at what happened to her and why it happens. Not the author!! There was no chance for a happy ending once she volunteered. No one walking into that situation can be happy. That's why we find out how all the other tributes were living lives of psychological torment. It has taken my relative almost 10 years to start to put some of his life back together. This is reality. There are no happy endings in war. There are just endings, and you have to try to move on. You can find peace, but it takes time, and you can never go back to who you were.<br /><br />In the end, Katniss ended up trying to put war behind her, and in doing so she embraced love. This is symbolized by who she ends up with. It's the only possible outcome, the only choice, considering all she went through. Gale and Peeta represent two different lives, two different futures, war and love. It makes sense why Katniss gets mad at Gale. She is repelled from the fight in him because she can't handle being associated with any more death. I really commend Suzanne Collins for writing it the way she did. So while I am still feeling sick over it, I am also really impressed with it. Thank you for the wake up call. Hopefully it will help our children learn that war only destroys, and we have to find a better way if we are to survive.

B0007Y08IS|2.0|Team America: World Police (2004) is Trey Parker and Matt Stone's ambitious attempt to create an R-rated comedy composed of puppets and marionettes. Like the South Park Movie, Team America is a satire on American politics. And like that movie, it is simply not funny. In most cases, it's not even good.<br /><br />That's not to say Parker and Stone don't deserve credit for trying something new. In fact, Team America tries to be everything: a satire on the Don Simpson/Jerry Bruckheimer blockbusters, a swipe at the bloated action epics of Michael Bay, a comical reflection of jingoistic patriotism from Bush-Era America and a vilification of liberal Hollywood celebrities. And yet, with the exception of the first, TA fails in its supposedly satirical message and delivery of jokes.<br /><br />Many fans have compared Trey Parker and Matt Stone to the iconoclastic comedy troupe Monty Python. I beg to differ. Python were a group of sophisticated intellectuals whose mixture of cerebral humor, political incorrectness and witty dialogue helped produce seething, intelligent and increasingly relevant television episodes and movie satires. Parker and Stone are two practical jokers who think that going after virtually everybody is equivalent to smart satire. No, it does not, especially in a frustratingly haphazard movie like this.<br /><br />And what a frustratingly haphazard movie this is; an audacious idea that falters in uneven execution. The opening sequence, where Team America assault the terrorists and, in turn, annihilate Paris, is extremely funny and perfectly executed, both exciting in its action and mocking of the Bruckheimer productions that Parker and Stone are going after. The sequence where Gary tries to uncover terrorist information in Cairo and is caught in a car chase between the terrorists and Team America is both a loving homage to the Mos Eisley scene from [[ASIN:B0006VIE4C Star Wars]] and an effective jab at the gung-ho American politics of movies like [[ASIN:B0002WZTOI Top Gun]] and the [[ASIN:B0015XHP4A Rambo]] movies.<br /><br />And yet even with these two or three great sequences, the movie doesn't work. Team America is an irritating blend of unfunny jokes (\\"Matt Damon\\", showing loyalties via oral sex, the body part speech in the end), uninteresting puppeteering, mediocre screenwriting (you know this movie's in trouble when it needs to sing about Michael Bay to kill some time) and the longest vomiting scene of all time, which would be funny if it wasn't so tedious. Oh and there's a puppet sex scene. Ha ha ha. Not.<br /><br />This also leads to another problem with TA: the liberal Hollywood celebrities. Why are they in the movie? Why are they portrayed as villains? To be made fun of? That's it? That's not satire; that's grade school mockery. Instead of just mentioning it, why didn't Parker and Stone use Sean Penn's visit to Iraq as a political motivation for aligning themselves with Jong II? That would have given the movie's satirical message a greater edge and the movie more bite. And, sure, there is a subplot where Gary idolizes Alec Baldwin and is shocked to see him revile Team America as the scum of the earth. But it's never fully realized. The actor caricatures seem to exist to be made fun of and that's it. Just because you poke fun at something doesn't mean it is good satire. Yes, it's funny. Ha ha ha. But as satire, it's childish.<br /><br />It's unfortunate that so much talent was wasted on such a feeble movie. Besides Parker and Stone, the movie was photographed by Bill Pope, who did cinematography for [[ASIN:B000ICXQOM Army of Darkness]](1993) and the [[ASIN:B000OPPBEQ Matrix]] movies and the music was composed by Harry Gregson-Williams, who is also the conductor of several Metal Gear Solid video games; to speak little of the puppet operators, who had their talents squandered due to a lackluster script.<br /><br />TA deserves praise for being bold and trying to be creative. It's just too bad that the script couldn't deliver the goods. For a pair of supposedly iconoclastic comedy writers, it's depressing that the best jokes Parker and Stone could come up with involve hammers, oral sex and vomiting. If you want better satires, you're better off checking [[ASIN:B000055Y0X Dr. Strangelove, Or: How I Learned to Stop Worrying and Love the Bomb (Special Edition)]] (1964), [[ASIN:B001LMU19G The Exterminating Angel (The Criterion Collection)]] (1962), [[ASIN:B00006RCNV The King of Comedy]] (1983) and [[ASIN:6305922756 This is Spinal Tap (Special Edition)]] (1984). This movie will pass as a nice time-waster but nothing more.<br /><br />Recommendation to avoid.ldn't deliver the goods. This movie will pass as a nice time-waster but nothing more.<br /><br />Recommendation to avoid.

0307591549|1.0|This book is a manifesto for maintaing the status quo.  The thesis is based on the belief that industrial production is a necessary function of human society and that by changing mentalities and habits in order to make the worker \\"happier\\", it (production) can marginally be increased.  This is no different than a pill... Mr. Archor fails to identify the pathology of psychosis.  He assumes that the unfortunate reality of individual unhappiness is inherent - that the negative mentalities, destructive and perverse dispositions are the problems not symptoms.  I wonder if he thinks that if everyone read his book and magically all became \\"happy\\" (despite their truly meaningless labor, spiritual depravity and detached, mediated aberration ironically referred to as life) that a better humanity would prevail. This book is Huxley's idea of soma; its not promoting creativeness, individuality, and actual happiness. It might as well be titled, \\"how to increase robotic functioning\\", or \\"happy sheep make better food\\".  Maybe the reason people aren't happy with their jobs, are depressed, pessimistic etc. is because being a facet of production and having your very existence negotiated and judged by its economic fecundity is fundamentally alienating.  The tone of this book is regurgitated, simplistic, mechanical and short.  I don't care that the author went to harvard however badly he seemingly wants me to;  somehow he apparently didn't learn how to write in his ten years there... reference scientific experiment, personal anecdote, individual application, repeat.  There are lots of really amazing topics presented in this book but mostly glossed over so as to fit in 209 pages.  I understand it's primarily marketed to the advertising executive who's flight is delayed, sitting in the La Guardia barns and noble but don't make it so obvious.  Bright cover, big letters, few pages - I knew happiness would be in there! At least there wasn't a huge picture of the author in his harvard t-shirton the cover. I'd ask for my money back but I'd rather the author use it to buy and read any Erich Fromm work.<br /><br />One last note: what I dislike most about this book is the disservice done to the real sources of the most artistic, inventive, genius revolutionaries of history.  The greatest works of music, art and literature were not born from casual contentment and perfunctory joy; and their creators were not the good little producers that this book espouses for. Degenerate anger, hopeless depression, suffering and sadness awaken the inspiration for revolution and change. I'm not saying that everyone should want to be depressed, but more so just that its o.k. if we are; being dissatisfied with this artificial global culture is natural and trying to be happy about it isn't going to make it or us better.

0151013837|5.0|I usually don't have good luck reading books that just about everyone else had read. But Li of Pi--what a surprise. Published in 2001, and read by nearly everyone since, I came upon the book in a used bookstore. I loved the smell of used books, the feel of them--they're always better if they have signs of history: writings in the margins, wears and tears, here and there. This one was in good shape, meaning the reader before me had not left any discernible markings.<br /><br />I picked out the book because I needed something and wasn't quite sure where to look. All I could remember was someone telling me once upon a time that it was about a person alone in a boat with a tiger. Honestly, I thought that it was one of those clever post-modern books. Something I would try to write when I was 23 and considered myself an \\"artist.\\"<br /><br />But no, the book is about a person on a boat stranded in the Pacific, and despite the absurdity, the person is caught with a tiger. And despite the odds, the book is all about the physicality and realism of the situation.<br /><br />The book is brutal, physical, detailed look at the will to survive. And yet because the protagonist is deep and spiritual, his journey to survive is also deep and spiritual. The author demonstrates the infiniteness of the natural world and the wonder of existence in the minute details of Pi Patel's tale of survival. In the effort to survive, every detail of the book takes on new significance.<br /><br />The form of the book also says quite a lot about the state of modern best sellers. I'm told from the outset that quite a lot of important newspapers and magazines liked the book. How on earth did the Economist come to review the book? That's my question. It's a magazine about economics and politics for cripes sake.<br /><br />I couldn't imagine a better traveling book. I wasn't traveling when I read the book, but the book itself has motivated me to do more traveling. It also makes you treasure life, family, God, the food in front of you, and to behold the wonder of the natural world around you.<br /><br />Which parts did I like the best? Without giving too much away, the beginning--Yann Martel's story of the origin of the book, the moments that they character has alone with the tiger of course, the excitement of the island, and the ambiguous ending.<br /><br />I'll admit it. I'm not smart enough to know what the ending means. \\"And so it is with God\\" remains a cryptic line in my mind. I will leave it to you to explain it to me.<br /><br />I think that reading group guides at the end of books are hilarious--they are meant to appeal to those readers out there who are not cynical about group reading (never tried a book club--but will one day). And yet, despite my cynicism I am baffled by the simple complexity of the questions.<br /><br />Is Life of Pi a tragedy, romance, or comedy? It has aspects of all of them for sure--but it has a happy ending without ever having to be a romance or a comedy. A spiritual triumph-book! It cannot be called a comedy. Even the most absurd parts of the book happen in utmost seriousness. What laughs we receive only relieve us from the horror of the protagonist's tale. Romance--between a boy and a tiger? Sort of. Pi really did love his tiger friend, didn't he? And the protagonist appears like a jealous lover at the end (hopefully, that didn't give away too much).<br /><br />Aw, life is nothing but hellos and goodbyes. So until next time, I say to you, whoever may read this: good journey.God, the food in front of you, and to behold the wonder of the natural world around you.<br /><br />Which parts did I like the best? B0074V3712|4.0|Wilson soon identifies the creation myth as \\"the Darwinian device for survival,\\" and in doing so he's actually taking a giant step in the right direction. However, he doesn't recognize the similarity of all creation myths. Instead he adopts Walter Burkert's explanations of sacrifice as emerging from \\"the hunt,\\" as though we're the only creatures that hunt, or the only creatures that hunt socially. But virtually all creation myths have a hero at the nexus who is sacrificed in order that \\"the world\\" can come into being. They may describe this world differently, but that's always related to local conditions. What's important is that in myths there is seldom a direct reference to the violence associated with this sacrifice. It survives only in myth's co-traveler: ritual. But why is it that Wilson seems blind to this unifying theme behind all myth? Could it be that he, as we, simply resist the notion that human culture is always and only founded on murder? This would, no doubt, be a bad reflection on *us* as a species immersed in a universe with no teleological principle, since we not only haven't transcended the founding murder, but fail to even recognize it when the evidence is everywhere.<br /><br />But how else than a peculiar universal amnesia could we, together, \\"arrive at the place where we began, and recognize it for the first time?\\"

0930073193|4.0|Economics in One Lesson might be Hazlitt's most famous work, largely thanks to its readability and enduring relevance.<br /><br />The book is divided into thirty-five short chapters, about four and a half pages each.  The first and last chapters state and re-state, respectively, the \\"lesson\\" for which the book is named: that when making economic decisions, we must \\"consider what their effect would be in the long run on all groups,\\" rather than focusing only on particular groups, at a particular time.  The point is to think through the full, long-term implications of any particular policy, for all groups, everywhere, particularly the \\"invisible\\" implications.<br /><br />Hazlitt's writing style is perfectly readable for the non-economist, and some of his linguistic innovations are downright brilliant.  I will be using \\"multiple economic personality disorder,\\" for example, and \\"wageer,\\" much more often.  The parable of the broken window is also quite useful, and even more beautiful returning to it a second time, after having finished the book.<br /><br />The Lesson is also enduringly relevant.  It was first published in 1946, and last revised in 1978, but many passages seem like they could have been plucked from this morning's Wall Street Journal.  Chapters like \\"Saving the X Industry,\\" \\"The Drive for Exports,\\" \\"How the Price System Works,\\" \\"Minimum Wage Laws,\\" \\"The Function of Profits,\\" and \\"The Assault on Saving,\\" among others, seem eerily contemporary.<br /><br />Hazlitt must have felt the same d&eacute;j&agrave; vu when he last revised this book.  He observes in the final chapter of this edition (published in 1979) that virtually none of the lessons from the first edition (published in 1946) had been absorbed by policymakers in the intervening decades.  That policymakers seem to have absorbed so little (even as some have over-absorbed some of it, e.g. on taxes) since 1979 makes me all the more suspicious that The Lesson will continue to relevant decades from now.<br /><br />Still, other discussions are dated, and some seem backwards.  For example, while the logic underlying Hazlitt's concerns about tariffs, rent controls, the \\"fetish\\" of full employment, the danger of inflation, and so on still seems largely correct, today these issues are at best relatively unimportant, and at worst the opposite of what most need attention right now.  Tariffs have fallen dramatically since World War II, for example, rent is if anything too high, hardly anybody is actually opposed to labor-saving technological advances any more, inflation is almost certainly less important than an economy in its third straight year of 8%+ unemployment, and so on.<br /><br />More fundamentally, Hazlitt's arguments are almost entirely unempirical.  He seems content throughout The Lesson to base his opinions merely on logic, common sense, and a few assumptions, rather than the sort of careful empirical work that have since added considerable nuance to economist' understanding of minimum wage laws, the role savings, inflation rates, etc.<br /><br />Also, in a few places The Lesson's readability comes at the expense of accuracy.  Too often Hazlitt uses a term like \\"only,\\" \\"virtually all,\\" or some other superlative, where the truth is really much more subtle and nuanced.  His fear of inflation and praise for savings, for example, seem limitless, in contrast to his more balanced approach to unions.<br /><br />In Hazlitt's defense, he probably over-stated some of his points for effect (Paul Krugman does the same thing), especially towards the end of the book.  I suspect his true opinions were far more skeptical and nuanced than his most hyperbolic sentences make it seem.<br /><br />All things considered, I strongly recommend Economics in One Lesson.  Virtually every chapter is at least somewhat relevant today, if not highly so, an impressive feat for a book last revised 34 years ago.  And I suspect that \\"multiple economic personality disorder\\" will eventually make even the least relevant chapters important once again.<br /><br />Hazlitt's opinions deserve to be questioned, criticized, and perhaps even been refuted, but \\"the lesson, and the underlying logic on which it is based, deserves to be taken quite seriously.<br /><br />I suggest reading the first and last chapters, then taking a look at the table of contents, and reading whatever piques your interest.relevant chapters important once again.<br /><br />Hazlitt's opinions deserve to be questioned, criticized, and perhaps even been refuted, but \\"the lesson, and the underlying logic on which it is based, deserves to be taken quite seriously.<br /><br />I suggest reading the first and last chapters, then taking a look at the table of contents, and reading whatever piques your interest.
