1596432594|5.0|The first thing you see on the cover is a quote from Feynman's mother Lucille after Omni named him the world's smartest man. \\"If that's the worlds smartest man, god help us.\\"  This graphic novel does a good job of hitting the main parts of Feynman's life.  The authors also take on the challenge of explaining Feynman's very mathematical insights in graphic form.<br /><br />In particular they develop his explanation of how electrons and light interact. This doesn't explain gravity or radioactivity, but it does explain most observable phenomena, all the interactions taking place outside atoms. Our understanding increases and we get extremely good agreement between this QED, Quantum Electrodynamics theory, and experiment. No experiment contradicts it. So it works.  It is very strange, but that makes it intriguing and interesting, even if we don't understand it.  This is the way nature works the predictions agree with experiment, but we don't understand why it works.<br /><br />Light sometimes acts like a wave, other times a particle- a photon, both are found by experiment.  Feynman prefers to use photons. For example take photons passing through a window, and some photons are reflected back.  If you visualize a single pane of glass with photons striking the front surface about 4% of the photons reflect off this surface, the rest of the photons continue thru the glass and hit the back side of the glass and another 4% of the photons are reflected and the rest continue outside of the glass. For a single color of light (from a laser) all the photons are exactly the same-why do a small number reflect?  The 4% is quite predictable, but for individual photons not predictable at all.  Feynman doesn't understand why, doesn't like it, but is fascinated by it. This is where the combination of the artist's graphics with the attached bubble text makes it clearer as an explanation.<br /><br />Another example is a soap bubble or oil on a water surface that show color.  but different parts show different colors. It depends on the thickness of the surface of the soap bubble and when the light reflects off the front surface and the back surface the light that reflects off the back surface has to travel a little bit further to get to your eye.  Different colors of light will interact differently.  If the conditions are right the photons add together and you see that color (constructive interference). If they subtract then that color disappears (destructive interference) and that color disappears.  You see the resultant of the colors that remain.<br /><br />Feynman goes deeper by using vectors to represent each reflection from the front and back surface. He uses a graph with time in the vertical direction and distance travelled in the horizontal direction, and connects the vectors head to tail. This can be done for each possible reflection and each is connected to the last head to tail. The angle and length of each vector contains information, connecting the vectors head to tail keeping their angle the same (most important), and using a final vector to connect the beginning tail to the final head gives the final vector whose length can be considered one side of a square.  The area of the square is just this vector length (the length of a side of the square) times itself, and is the probability of seeing the light beam taking this path.  Adding up all the arrows gives the probability for the beam of light, and it works for electrons, as well as photons.  Light takes the path that requires the least amount of time.  Light is transmitted through glass by photons being absorbed by electrons and reemitted in the same direction. Light is reflected when the photons are absorbed and reemitted in a different direction.  This absorption of a photon and reemission takes time and is why photons seem to travel more slowly through a media such as glass or air.  The velocity of the photon is the same when moving through water, but the interactions with electrons take time so the photons appear to be moving slower because it takes longer to move through the water.<br /><br />If you draw an electron going back in time it describes a positron or antielectron, this is the particle used in PET scans.  Interesting!<br /><br />The examples are much easier to understand when a talented artist has clear graphs accompanying the explanation.  The authors include a very interesting bibliography for continued reading with links to where you can see videos with Feynman talking about a variety of topics.

A \\"Feast for Crows\\" focuses only on the characters in Kings Landing and the Seven Kingdoms, leaving out several other important characters in the rest of the series.  The main theme of this book is about watching characters, good and evil, grow stronger and truer characters.  Between Sam Tarly, Jon Snow, Jaime Lannister, and Littlefinger, everyone is stepping up to the bat in this game of thrones.<br /> Jaime Lannister and Littlefinger, not known for their human compassion or understanding seem to have turned a new leaf in \\"A Feast for Crows\\".  With the loss of his sword hand, Jaime has lost what men fear/respect him for the most and is therefore forced to focus on his wits and leadership.  After Sansa Stark escaped from Kings Landing, Jaime not only sends Brienne of Tarth to fulfill his oath to Catelyn Stark, but he provides her with a new valerian sword, (Oathbreaker), a sack of gold, and a royal declaration from King Tommen that she is on official royal business.  Jaime is not only doing his best to fulfill his oath to Catelyn Stark to protect her children, but he is also showing respect to Brienne, something that she is in short supply of.  Littlefinger also appears to care for someone other than himself.  He helps Sansa escape, under the guise of his natural born daughter, and takes to protecting her. Both characters carve morally better identities for themselves in this fourth installment of the series.<br /> Jon Snow finds himself in quite the predicament in \\"A Feast of Crows\\".  With the offer from King Stannis to reclaim his Stark heritage, he is tempted to abandon The Wall when he is needed most.  However, he is elected into a different role of power and loses his chance to finally become Ned Starks true child.  With his new role he quickly changes and hardens himself. B00633PFQC|4.0|What Money Can't Buy: The Moral Limits of Markets by Michael J. Sandel<br /><br />\\" What Money Can't Buy\\" is the thought-provoking book that asks the ethical question, \\"Are there some things that money can buy but shouldn't?\\" With a plethora of fascinating examples, best-selling author and famed Harvard professor Michael J. Sandel once again dazzles the mind with philosophical mind teasers. In this enlightening edition, Sandel challenges the reader with economic ethics, are economic markets replacing our moral judgments? Sandel insists that these are questions that society needs to answer and decide what values should govern our social and civic life. What sets Sandel apart is precisely his ability to ask thought-provoking questions and provide lucid perspectives. This 245-page book is composed of the following five chapters: 1. Jumping the Queue, 2. Incentives, 3. How Markets Crowd Out Morals, 4. Markets in Life and Death, and 5. Naming Rights.<br /><br />Positives:<br />1. Elegant, conversational tone that makes this book a treat to read.<br />2. As thought-provoking a book as you will find.<br />3. So many fascinating economic topics covered in a brief book.<br />4. Philosophy made fun. Sandel writes with panache.<br />5. So easy to understand yet so profound.<br />6. Very even-handed approach. Does a great job of addressing issues from different perspectives.<br />7. Sandel challenges you to think. His trademark engaging style draws you in and just when you thought you had it all figured out he forces you to rethink your position. Excellent!<br />8. A great job of defining the role of our markets.<br />9. A master at providing countless examples of modern moral dilemmas.<br />10. The creative minds of the free markets...interesting business models. Line standing business applied to several businesses as a curious example.<br />11. Some examples will test your moral fiber. I'm not going to spoil it.<br />12. Thought-provoking questions abound, \\"Under what conditions do market reflect freedom of choice, and under what conditions do they exert a kind of coercion?\\"<br />13. An interesting look at education and pay for grades programs.<br />14. Health bribes...do they work?<br />15. Perverse situations...what would you do?<br />16. The morality of environmental preservation, climate change, endangered species.<br />17. Great quotes, \\"Morality represents the way we would like to work, and economics represents how it actually does work.\\"<br />18. What money can and cannot buy and why. Great stuff.<br />19. A fascinating look at the \\"value\\" of life. Enlightening.<br />20. The naming rights chapter goes over the business and ethics of paying for ads in practically every aspect of our lives.<br />21. Being the big baseball fan that I am I was happy to see a couple of sections on baseball.<br />22. The two running objections of laissez-faire argument: coercion and unfairness. Plenty of examples.<br />23. Insight into public marketing.<br />24. The positives and negatives of commercialism.<br />25. Comprehensive notes section.<br /><br />Negatives:<br />1. So good it was too brief...I wanted more.<br />2. Perhaps not as great as Sandel's previous book: \\"Justice: What's the Right Thing to Do?\\" but it is still an excellent book.<br />3. Folks on opposite side of the political spectrum may have something to complain about and that may be a good thing.<br />4. Surprised there wasn't many shenanigans from Wall Street. That would have taken several books though.<br />5. Not a ground breaking book just better asked questions.<br /><br />In summary, I enjoyed this book, it will give you topics to discuss for years to come. What sets Sandel apart is his innate ability to ask interesting questions and provide well thought out answers. Few authors have that innate ability to draw you in and make you ponder your arguments. The book has few shortcomings including the fact that is indeed a short book and a lot may in fact be logical to many. A 4.5star book out of five. That being said, if you are looking for a philosophical book that is a treat to read, \\"What Money Can't Buy\\" is worth every penny. I highly recommend it!<br /><br />Further suggestions: The excellent, \\"[[ASIN:0374532508 Justice: What's the Right Thing to Do?]]\\" by Michael J. B005S9EJZA|4.0|I have this subversive image of Nicholas Sparks in a wifebeater at the computer, angrily pounding the keyboards and listening to death metal while a burly biker gives him a tat. Because a guy can't be that wholesome and sensitive, can he? Except that, like clockwork, Sparks' bestselling novels keep getting made into films. THE LUCKY ONE is merely the latest adaptation.<br /><br />This is Hollywood, babe, so we shouldn't harp on them heaps of uncanny coincidences. Such as these: In Iraq, the morning after a harrowing exchange of gunfire with the enemy, U.S. Marine Sergeant Logan Thibault (Zac Efron) gets off his duff and picks up a photo he glimpses lying in the rubble. Suddenly, there's an explosion right where he had been sitting, and that's when the notion probably first struck him that the photo is some sort of lucky charm. Eight months later, Logan's third tour of duty ends and he's going home, that photo still tucked away in his keeping. Logan regards the mysterious girl in the photo as his guardian angel, someone who'd kept him safe and breathing. He wants to find her and thank her. But he doesn't know who she is.<br /><br />Back home, Logan Thibault is unable to settle down, and off he goes with his dog, walking, just walking. In his wanderings, he crosses several states and eventually ends up at a family-run dog kennel in Louisiana. See, he'd never stopped trying to look for that girl in the photo. By pure happenstance, the girl in question, whose name is Beth (Taylor Schilling), runs the kennel and she assumes that Logan has shown up looking for work. And because the plot demands it, Logan rides with the deception.<br /><br />The kennel is this run-down establishment, and Logan quickly makes himself useful, never mind Beth's bitter ex-husband's assertion that he may be a \\"crazy drifter.\\" It shouldn't surprise viewers that THE LUCKY ONE follows the patented Nicholas Sparks formula. The film, lushly romantic and graced with evocative cinematography, reminds me quite a bit, in terms of tone and sheer escapism, of [[ASIN:B00006ZXSI A Walk in the Clouds]]. Of course, it makes more sense for Logan to right away inform Beth of why he's really there. But where would the movie be? Taylor Schilling is sweet and appealing, playing a young single mom raising a child while fending off the unwanted affections of her influential ex-husband (who is this bullying deputy sheriff). Beth nurses her own grief. She's yet to come to terms with her brother's death during the war. Logan's military past upsets her and prejudices her against him. Plus, he seems to be everywhere she's at. It's irritating. We all know where this is going. But Nicholas Sparks, with his sweaty wifebeater shirt and his deep aversion to sunlight, is adept at manipulating his audience.<br /><br />Zac Efron surprises me. It's evident that those HIGH SCHOOL MUSICAL pictures are increasingly dimming in the rear view. A friend recommended CHARLIE ST. CLOUD to me a while ago, and I laughed at her with the smug convinction of someone who views Zac Efron as someone who belongs strictly in the teeny bopper crowd. But Efron demonstrates reserve and maturity in his portrayal of a troubled jarhead desperate for solace. He plays Logan as a man of serious humor but with hidden depths. I liked him. Blythe Danner comes in as Schilling's wise grandmother. Danner, naturally, exhibits that infallible knack for sensing the right man for her hurting granddaughter. I long for a grandmother like Danner. It surely would save time and effort on them online dating services.<br /><br />A manly man like me can readily admit that he's a romantic cuss. I enjoyed THE LUCKY ONE quite a bit. Nothing in the story surprised me. I looked each plot swerve (not many) squarely in the eye. But Efron and Schilling invest in their characters, and they effortlessly pull the viewer in. It helps that the little boy is likable. There's a stillness and thoughtfulness to the film which I appreciated. It doesn't indulge in too many grand gestures, although, okay, the climactic sequence is pretty melodramatic. 0205313426|4.0|A surprisingly entertaining as well as useful little book. Good writing is about \\"plainness, simplicity, orderliness, sincerity\\", it says. Writers who seek to follow trends are \\"as good as dead\\", it warns. And the queasy are advised to speak of feeling \\"nauseated\\" not \\"nauseous\\" - unless they're sure they \\"have that effect on others\\". A lot of good advice has been published by writers over the years, Mark Twain, George Orwell and Elmore Leonard among them. For me, this paragraph from The Elements of Style is as good as any of it: \\"Vigorous writing is concise. A sentence should contain no unnecessary words, a paragraph no unnecessary sentences, for the same reason that a drawing should have no unnecessary lines and a machine no unnecessary parts. This requires not that the writer make all sentences short or avoid all detail and treat subjects only in outline, but that every word tell.\\"

0195145925|4.0|It is safe to say that time is one of the most perplexing of phenomena to challenge the human mind. No surprise then that it's been the subject of some of the most momentous conjectures and research findings, from Minkowski's time, to Einstein's time dilation and Stephen Hawking's \\"imaginary time\\".<br /><br />Then there is the question of whether time, as an objective property of the cosmos, exists at all. This is the subject taken up in this book, by Julian Barbour.  Barbour himself is somewhat of an enigma as a physicist, not having followed the usual rigorous \\"publish or perish\\" path. Evidently, though \\"doing a Ph.D. on Einstein's theory of gravitation\\" he opted for the path of a Russian scientific literature translator which afforded him the latitude for other pursuits - such as time.<br /><br />Not many physicists would have made such an odd turn, mainly because most of them would have followed the traditional path, as opposed to being language translators. But the odd detour is precisely what put Barbour in the place to write the book he has.<br /><br />Is this a physics text? Perusing the chapters it has the veneer of a physics text as one peruses the assorted 22 chapters plus the Epilogue, though to be sure - several of the chapters (Chapters 2, 4 and especially 7 - `Paths in Platonia' and 17- `The Philosophy of Timelessness' have the look and feel of philosophy.<br /><br />Indeed, reading through many of the other sections to do with special and general relativity (Part Three, Chapters 8-11) echoed earlier issues and topics from Hans Reichenbach's 'The Philosophy of Space and Time' (Dover, 1958).<br /><br />Compare for example, Barbour's Chapter 3 (`A Timeless World') and the section `Is Motion Real?' (p. 46) with Reichenbach's `Unreal Sequences' (p. 147) in which he notes (p. 149):<br /><br />\\"The discussion of unreal sequences shows clearly the significance of the causal theory of time. Only the events in a real causal process are temporally ordered: unreal sequences obtain their time order as a result of some method of time comparison which is already defined within the system.\\"<br /><br />And of course, in Barbour's Chapter 7 (`Paths to Platonia') we see this very modus operandi in terms of defining \\"Machian histories\\" in the context of the distance between any two neighboring points in Platonia- leading to Barbour's Platonian geodesics (pp. 116-1119) and \\"one of the two prime pieces of evidence for suggesting the universe is timeless\\". (The other arising from quantum mechanics, i.e. in its acausal foundations)<br /><br />Not surprisingly, therefore, a hard core reductionist physicist reading this text will not be too impressed and treat it more as philosophy than physics. On the other hand, on reading it I treated it in just this way which is why I awarded it 4 stars.<br /><br />Why the tendency for physicists to dismiss it? Well, mainly because if you remove conventional time (and by extension, motion) from our formalist gestalt you essentially remove the basis for doing most physics- which let's face it, is mainly embodied in differential equations of the form:<br /><br />d(p)/ dt =  m (dv/dt) = ma<br /><br />Thus, if one reads Barbour's text strictly as a physics book, with the expectations of such, one will more likely be disappointed.<br /><br />Never mind that the timeless Zeitgeist isn't as alien as we'd like to believe.<br /><br />For example, G.J. Whittrow, in 'The Nature of Time', (1972, Pelican Books, Great Britain, p. 103), observes:<br /><br />\\"the passage of time is merely to be regarded as a feature of consciousness that has no objective counterpart.\\"<br /><br />And more recently, a special issue of Scientific American on Time (pages 8 and 15), included an article: 'Is Time an Illusion?' by Craig Callender, who observes (p. 16):<br /><br />\\"Many in theoretical physics have come to believe that time fundamentally does not exist\\".<br /><br />His article and one by Paul Davies (p. 8) are definitely worth reading for the temporal objectivists!<br /><br />Other physicists, e.g. Robert Nadeau, Menos Kafatos et al, have correctly observed that since time itself is susceptible to certain formulations of the Heisenberg Uncertainty Principle (e.g. its energy-time presentation) then time itself is uncertain, and if uncertain, no defined units or dimensions can be assigned. This also means time is elastic. It can bend, expand or contract, and even do so under the influence of consciousness. (See, `Conscious Mind in the Physical World', by Euan Squires)<br /><br />Then there is the realm of \\"imaginary time\\" and \\"imaginary space\\". For example, stay where you are and let one minute elapse on your watch. You have performed a 'movement in time' without a corresponding movement in space. We say you have traversed imaginary space. This imaginary space can easily be computed:<br /><br />Im(x) = i(300,000 km/s x 60 s) = 18,000,000(i) km<br /><br />That is, you have traversed 18 million imaginary kilometers or 11.25 million miles in imaginary space. (Im(x) is the symbolic representation for an imaginary space (x) transition).<br /><br />My point in belaboring all this is that there are many different time perspectives, and who is to say which one is absolutely the correct form, or usage? What does `dt' in our differential equations really mean? If objective time or a unit of measure, how truly objective is it? Can we dismiss any hint of elasticity or subjectivity?<br /><br />If one adopts the right mental framework, in other words, there is no reason not to actually enjoy Barbour's book and presentation - if one can admit of a bit more flexibility in time's definitions while doing so!rth reading for the temporal objectivists!<br /><br />Other physicists, e.g. Robert Nadeau, Menos Kafatos et al, have correctly observed that since time itself is susceptible to certain formulations of the Heisenberg Uncertainty Principle (e.g. its energy-time presentation) then time itself is uncertain, and if uncertain, no defined units or dimensions can be assigned. (Im(x) is the symbolic representation for an imaginary space (x) transition).<br /><br />My point in belaboring all this is that there are many different time perspectives, and who is to say which one is absolutely the correct form, or usage? What does `dt' in our differential equations really mean? If objective time or a unit of measure, how truly objective is it? Can we dismiss any hint of elasticity or subjectivity?<br /><br />If one adopts the right mental framework, in other words, there is no reason not to actually enjoy Barbour's book and presentation - if one can admit of a bit more flexibility in time's definitions while doing so!

In his dreams, his wife and young daughter are in terrible danger. The film sometimes blurs the line between fantasy and reality and we see events unfold that are later revealed as dreams. He also hears the storm approaching. While he is working outside with his colleague, Dewart (Shea Whigham), Curtis hears a thunderbolt strike. Dewart doesn't hear a thing.<br /><br />Do you trust what you see and hear without question? What would you think if you could hear things that nobody else could?<br /><br />If what Curtis experiences is really happening, how is that possible? Is it some form of intervention from God? It is some kind of psychic ability? Is the subconscious mind revealing what Curtis knows on some level?<br /><br />To complicate matters, Curtis was left alone outside a store as a child when his mother had a mental breakdown and just walked away. He visits her in a nursing home because he fears that he might be going down the same path. It's such a realistic possibility that we are drawn into his world. Is he going crazy? He seems rational most of the time, but then he experiences another dream or hallucination.<br /><br />His wife, Samantha (Jessica Chastain), is more than patient. We get the feeling that their relationship is strong and she clearly loves him. In a good relationship, it should be possible to talk openly about such problems, but Curtis initially hides everything from her. This is done out of fear. He's afraid of what might be happening to him and he's embarrassed about his experiences. He visits a doctor and a psychiatrist without her knowledge and even makes a serious financial decision without discussing it.<br /><br />Lack of communication is a huge problem in most relationships. If you are with the person you are supposed to trust more than anyone in the world, why can't you voice all of your fears? Curtis is eventually compelled to talk to Samantha because there's no other way to explain his bizarre behavior.<br /><br />Take Shelter shows us what occurs in the mind of a tormented man. The tension slowly builds and we can sense that something is going to happen. Is Curtis going crazy, or will all of his actions be justified? B006CS7UXO|4.0|PSB's second B-side compilation is very good, obviously, though not quite as good as Alternative.  The biggest difference to me is that the element of discovery is absent here.  When I bought Alternative in 1995, nearly every track was new for me.  Those B-sides would have been very difficult to find in the pre-commercial internet age, especially in the United States.  You would have spent an enormous amount of time and money ordering import singles.  So Alternative was just a godsend for PSB fanatics; we were offered two whole discs of songs that many people probably didn't know existed.<br /><br />But Format covers a different era.  I think most PSB fans will already know many if not most of the tracks here.  There were only a few that I hadn't heard.  With Bilingual in 1996, PSB started a policy of two-disc releases for all new albums that included associated B-sides and remixes on the second disc.  Those versions were limited-run, but they were widely available in the US.  Certainly not every track on Format was available on a double-CD release, but many were.  On the other hand, it's great to have all these tracks in one collection.<br /><br />Anyway, the music here is mostly great.  PSB were quick to point out on Alternative that not every track was good; there were some truly bad songs there, as there are here.  But the good-to-bad ratio is high and approximately equal to that album.  And like Alternative, the great thing about these tracks is that PSB experimented and stretched out.  The best tracks here come from the later years.  My favourites in no order:<br /><br />- How I Learned to Hate Rock & Roll<br />- The Boy Who Couldn't Keep His Clothes On<br />- The View From Your Balcony (one of their all-time best B-sides)<br />- Silver Age<br />- Always (a truly great track; seems to be one of NT's favourites too)<br />- Nightlife (great outtake from that album)<br />- Between Two Islands<br />- We're the Pet Shop Boys (ironically a cover)<br />- Transparent(energetic tech-house)<br />- The Resurrectionist<br />- Girls Don't Cry<br />- Gin and Jag (PSB's ode to aging. Favourite line: \\"Be careful with the decanter, dear.  Do you know how much it's worth?\\")<br />- After the Event (the lyrics are so great and so PSB)<br />- Up and Down<br /><br />Ultimately, PSB fans are quite blessed.  PSB are just a machine that cranks out an endless supply of great new pop songs.  There's a lot of music to work through here, and it really allows the listener a good idea the breadth and quality of music that PSB are capable of.

0393338827|5.0|Michael Lewis has written another masterpiece, combining financial history and machinations with intriguing characters. I read this without putting it down and irritated my girlfriend endlessly. I have been close enough to Wall Street insiders to question their honesty, intelligence, and motivation, but this book really puts it in perspective. The asset backed securities markets was gamed to fool investors and hide details from regulators. It would have been better to send people to jail than provide a bailout, but I guess some really are too big to fail.<br /><br />Investors were insuring mortgage bonds without any idea of equity positions, silent second mortgages, interest rate time bombs, or mortgagee creditworthiness. Sellers said the loss probability was 8% but it turned out to be 40% in some cases. Lewis tells the story of a handful of weirdos who had this figured out, some of whom still managed to end up losing. It took a while for our protagonists to figure out how to make money on this bet once they had determined that the house odds were all wrong. Eventually they introduced bond insurance products which were a better investment than a mixed bag of long and short bonds.  Going long and short does not work when all the bonds are toxic, as some Wall Streeters are still thinking about in their palatial retirement homes.<br /><br />What distinguishes Lewis from all the other chroniclers of this era is his ability to find interesting characters at the center of the story and tell it from their perspective. Here we have a depressed, loner, finally diagnosed with Asperberger's syndrome who saw it all coming from a bunker, and had to resort to questionable tactics to keep his investors in line until the bet paid off. Lewis also understands finance from his 1980's time on Wall Street and can explain financial instruments to the delight of those like me with Finance degrees.

B006OAB3ME|4.0|It's become one of those perennial pop star indulgences - doing an album of old standards.  Harry Nilsson set the bar very high with [[ASIN:B000EQ47WM A Little Touch of Schmilsson in the Night]], which has become a classic in its own right.  Since then, dozens of others have put on an old hat or a loose tie, stood in front of a microphone that Frank Sinatra once used, with arrangements that just scream, \\"Yo, I got me a big band!\\" and hoped that the history in the room would do the rest.  The albums that resulted are as much acting as singing, and not always good acting - a vanity piece dressed up as a tribute.<br /><br />Thankfully, this is not another album from a rock star playing dress up and giving us his versions (or even worse, \\"reinterpretations\\") of It Had to be You and I've Got You Under My Skin.  Paul McCartney is old enough to remember the days before rock and roll and most of the selections here reflect the music that was played in his home as he grew up rather than just dipping into the songbooks of Porter, Gershwin and Berlin.  This explains the choice of less widely known songs such as My Very Good Friend the Milkman.  I strongly suspect the Beatles' shared love of The Goon Show informed some of these selections.<br /><br />McCartney avoids another standards-album clich, and that is to pick a song in every imaginable style within the genre.  Instead, backed by Diana Krall and her band, he picks a vibe and sticks to it - and that vibe is most definitely lounge-bar-at-closing-time.  This is not an album intended for daylight hours.  The two new McCartney songs fit in seamlessly and unless you already know all the covers, you'd probably have a hard time picking them out.  My Valentine in particular has a beautiful melancholy about it.<br /><br />One could never accuse Paul McCartney of lacking versatility.  His previous album was a [[ASIN:B005H1IQ6M classical ballet]] and the one before that was an album of [[ASIN:B001GKYBXA ambient electronica]].  What this album focuses on is McCartney, the singer.  For the first time in a 50-year career, apart from a bit of acoustic guitar, Paul does nothing but sing.  Indeed, on some of the songs, he almost whispers rather than sing.  Again, if you didn't already know this was Paul McCartney, you might not immediately recognise his voice.<br /><br />If I have a criticism, it's that the flow of the record seems to get lost about three quarters of the way through.  Rather than concluding, it just sort of fizzles out.  The two extra tracks on the \\"deluxe\\" edition (why do they do this??) don't help this.<br /><br />There are those who want Paul McCartney to just keep belting out Get Back and Hey Jude for the rest of his life and this album will not please them.  If you think music began in 1964, just admit that this isn't your bag and wait for the next album.  As rock star indulgences go though, this is a pretty good one and a better than average example of the standards album.

B006ZCWU5K|5.0|Without a doubt, Wrecking Ball is the most confrontational album that Bruce Springsteen has done to date.  To me, it is music for our times, tapping into the anger that exists in America today. The lyrics are angry and point a finger at politicians and profiteers.  Songs like \\"Jack of All Trades\\" reflects on all of the stories in the news of the new working class in the aftermath of the Great Recession.  The mood is tired, broken but not defeated.  The economic crisis also comes through in \\"Death to My Hometown\\" where Wall Street rubs up against an ineffectual Congress. The Irish influenced music is a rallying cry to rise up and fight back.  \\"This Depression\\" provides a contrast to the anger of many of the songs in the need for love in a time of trouble.<br /><br />Not everything is entirely grim. \\"Land of Hope and Dreams,\\" originally released in 2001 is included on the disc gives a strong voice that things will be all right once again.  The brief saxophone solo by Clarence Clemons is a parting call from the past when things were much better, and will be again.  The special edition also includes two bonus tracks Swallowed Up and American Land. They provide a nice summing up of the album.<br /><br />Wrecking Ball is a powerful summing up of the America of today.  Springsteen gives voice to all of our hopes, fears and realities - what was lost and what people had to do to get on with life.  Maybe his message is one that some feel is misplaced but there is no denying that he has given us the feel of what it is like to be an American in the 21st century.  I agree with fellow reviewer George Whilhite that Springsteen is the John Steinbeck of rock.

B0072NWKEC|5.0|Okay, despite being an inveterate reader, I had somehow overlooked Philip Kerr. Luckily, the New York Times recently reviewed \\"Prague Fatale\\" favorably, so I took a chance on it. Wow. Just wow.<br /><br />Dense, beautifully written, wickedly smart, great dialog and scattered here and there literary references that fly by so fast one almost misses registering them. A moral guy in an immoral and evil universe, Bernie Gunther walks close to the edge always and somehow manages to not fall off. Let's resurrect Bogart and Bacall to star in this one!<br /><br />I won't recap the story - others seem to be doing that, the spoilers! - but I will say that the book is set mostly in Berlin in the run-up to World War II. Since I lived in Berlin for a few years in the mid-60s, I recognized the city and its landmarks absolutely. Kerr has not only done his research (see his afterword), but obviously spent time on the scene as well.<br /><br />I'm into \\"Field Gray\\" now. The war's over and Bernie's still in trouble. Dang, I'm going to have to do something like I did with Elmore Leonard ... read all of Philip Kerr's books in the order he wrote them. I can think of worse things to do.
